### Assistant [2026-07-08 15:57:51 UTC]

Yes — **Univers Next Pro** is Monotype's current digital release of Univers; for this design it's effectively the same typeface (same grotesque letterforms and proportions). It's the right family to enable.

You don't need most of that list. Here's what the design actually uses, measured across Home, Projects, and About:

- **Body, descriptions, nav, metadata** → Regular (weight 400)
- **Display headings and entry titles/labels** → Bold (the screenshots show clear bold, even though Figma's variable panel flattened everything to 400)
- **No italics** anywhere in the design
- **No condensed / compressed / extended** widths — it's all the standard (normal-width) family

So enable just these two from the base **Univers Next Pro** family:

- **Regular**
- **Bold**

That keeps the font payload lean (a core project principle) while covering every weight the design shows.

One optional consideration: the "Prada Index" wordmark and some entry titles *could* be **Medium** rather than Bold — at these sizes it's hard to be certain from the prototype. If you'd like a small hedge so I can fine-tune the wordmark/title weight during styling, add **Medium** as well (3 total). My recommendation is to start with **Regular + Bold** and add Medium only if we find we need it.

Which would you like?

## Summary

I've built the global style foundation for Prada Index — the workbench every block will sit on — measured Faithfully from three representative screens (Home, Projects, About):

- **Typography** — Univers Next Pro wired from your Adobe Fonts kit (`lnd2jia`), loaded early in the page head with a metric-matched Arial fallback to avoid layout shift. Regular 400 for body/nav/metadata, Bold 700 for display headings and titles. I mapped the measured editorial ramp (display 80px with tight −0.03em tracking, section/nav 37px, body 16px, tracked 14px labels, 13px dense table text) onto the EDS heading/body scale.
- **Color** — the strict monochrome palette: black on white, greys `#707070` (secondary/nav/borders), `#8d9091` (thin rule), `#eee` (image placeholders), `#f5f5f5` (light surface). Links are black, hovering to grey with an underline — no brand accent.
- **Layout & rhythm** — 1440px content max-width matching the Figma frames, 40px page padding, and a two-token vertical spacing system. Squared, uppercase, tracked buttons (no pill radius) to match the editorial tone.
- **Verification** — CSS lint passes clean and the design-token checker reports no findings. I recorded the full system in the design docs and captured the font-sourcing decision.

One thing surfaced: I found the extracted design data reports weight 400 everywhere, but the rendered screenshots clearly show Bold headings — so I honored the *rendered* weight, which is the correct Faithful call.

**One blocker to flag:** I couldn't visually confirm the foundation in the preview — the preview is currently showing a *different* site (a Semrush project), not your Prada repo, so my styles and the Univers font don't appear there yet. **Two questions before we continue:**

## Library-Handoff checklist (DA — your steps)

The library pages and `block-library.json` are in the repo, but four steps only you can do. **Steps 1–3 are required before the author panel shows anything; step 4 is separate.**

1. **Get the library pages into DA, then publish.** `block-library.json` serves from the code repo once committed, but the `content/library/*` pages live in DA — commit alone doesn't publish them. Bring them into DA (Console **Upload**, or author/paste in da.live at `/library/...`), then **Preview + Publish** the JSON and every library page.

2. **Add a CORS header** (the non-obvious one). Go to `https://tools.aem.live/tools/headers-edit/` → enter Organization `gabrielwalt` + Site `pr` → Fetch (a 404 is expected) → keep Path `/**` → Add Header: name `access-control-allow-origin`, value `*` → Save. Verify: `curl -I https://main--pr--gabrielwalt.aem.live/block-library.json | grep -i access-control`.

3. **Add the DA config row.** Open `https://da.live/config#/gabrielwalt/pr/` → **Add sheet**, name it exactly `library` → then:
   - Row 1 (headers): A1=`title`, B1=`path`, C1=`ref`
   - Row 2 (entry): A2=`Blocks`, B2=`/block-library.json`, C2=`main`
   - Save. (If it doesn't update after reload, the manifest is cached — hard-reload the manifest URL directly, then reload the editor.)

4. **Set the EMA "Library url"** (separate mechanism — read by me for block discovery/reuse, not the DA panel). Console **Settings → Project → "Library url"**. It usually points at a shared boilerplate library by default; only point it at this project's own library if you want *these* blocks as the reuse source. Since we have no blocks yet, you can leave it as-is for now.

**Success looks like:** after steps 1–3, opening the DA editor's Library panel shows a "Blocks" category. It'll be empty until we build Home's first block — at which point I'll add its library page and a `block-library.json` row in the same step.

### Assistant [2026-07-08 17:29:56 UTC]

Analysis recorded. Here's the complete breakdown.

## Home — in-depth analysis

**On your scroll question first, honestly:** I **cannot** observe the prototype's scroll behavior through the Figma tools — they only give me static frame screenshots and the node tree (sizes, positions, structure), not prototype interactions like pin/scroll. So I've *reconstructed* the mechanic from your description and cross-checked it against the frame stacking and exact measurements, which line up cleanly. I'll want to confirm it against the real prototype (or a screen recording) before finalizing the build.

### Structure — four stacked full-width layers (frame is 1440 × 4227.6)

1. **Hero** (720px) — full-bleed background image with white overlay content: a white-bordered metadata block top-left (Project / Type / Collaborators / Venue / Date rows, 13px), and two 80px display lines at the bottom ("Satellites II" / "Nicolas Winding Refn, Hideo Kojima").
2. **Index grid** (1471.6px, white) — the shared **header** (98.6px: "Prada Index" wordmark + grey 37px nav "Calendar Projects ⌕ … About" under a thin `#8d9091` rule) followed by a **4-column grid of 12 Entry Modules**. Each = a 331px square image + title (16px) + description + a grey type-tag ("Interview"/"Essay"). Row heights vary (392–431px) with description length.
3. **Issue banner** (511px) — this is a **dark section**: black bg, white text, "Issue 1 / Fall 2026", credits, the inverted white-fill ABOUT button, and a magazine mockup image. This maps directly onto the dark section we already built and verified.
4. **Page Content** (1525px, white) — a search bar plus the dense **data table**: ~55 rows × 6–7 columns, 13px uppercase grey with 1px borders.

### The scroll interaction (your spec, reconstructed)

**Phase 1 — hero pinned, white sheet rises over it.** The 720px hero stays fixed and doesn't move; the white index section scrolls *up over* it on a higher stacking layer, progressively covering it. The hero acts as a stationary backdrop.

**Phase 2 — white section's top row goes sticky.** Once the white section's top reaches the viewport top, its **header row pins** while the entry grid scrolls up *behind* it. Your key detail — "sticky height = top of white bg to the first image" — resolves to exactly the header band, **≈98.6px** (the header plus its 40/20 padding, everything above the first entry image). I'll measure the precise header-bottom → first-image-top delta at build to lock it.

**Build implications:** two independent sticky contexts stacked — the hero (`sticky`/`fixed; top:0`) must release *behind* the white header's own `sticky; top:0`. The classic trap: `overflow:hidden` on an ancestor kills `position:sticky`, so I'll use `overflow-x:clip` (per `css-pitfalls-eds`). This is bespoke Home behavior — a small CSS+JS treatment, not a reusable variant — and I'll build it respecting reduced-motion and test it on touch.

I also confirmed and closed one loose end: the section-metadata gap is fixed, so the Issue banner's dark treatment already works.

**One question before I build** — to get the pin/release timing exactly right:

### User [2026-07-08 17:49:29 UTC]

Amend the scroll model — the reveal mechanic isn't a one-off hero treatment,
it repeats at a second boundary, and there's a header row you haven't
accounted for yet:

Confirmed correct: Phase 1 (hero fixed, white grid section rises over it,
higher stacking layer) and Phase 2 (grid's nav row goes sticky, top: 0,

once the dark section's rising edge reaches the top). The dark section
itself carries no sticky header — once it has fully evicted the nav, it
scrolls away normally like ordinary content.

Then a FOURTH beat: once the table section (below the dark Issue banner)
reaches the top, its own header row — the "SEARCH … CLEAR" toolbar +
column headers (Project/Title/Type/Collaborator/Venue/Date/Producer) —
becomes a new position: sticky; top: 0 context and stays pinned for the
rest of the page.

So the mechanism is a repeating pattern, not a single special case:
- Reveal #1: white grid section evicts the fixed hero.
- Sticky #1: grid's nav row, pinned until evicted.
- Reveal #2: dark Issue section evicts the Phase 2 nav (same overlay
  technique as reveal #1, but this section has no sticky content of its own).
- Sticky #2: table's own header row, pinned for the remainder of the page.

1. Confirm you'll implement the dark-section eviction with the SAME
   overlay/z-index technique as the hero reveal, not by relying on the
   nav's sticky container simply ending in normal flow — the video shows an
   explicit cover-up, not a passive release.
2. Measure the reveal-#2 boundary (where the dark section's rising edge
   reaches the nav) the same way you're locking the reveal-#1 and Phase-2
   header-height numbers.
3. Reconfirm whether any of this (the dark-section eviction, or the table
   header sticky) needs to be shared with Calendar/Projects/Search/About
   via the common Page Template, versus staying bespoke to Home.

### Assistant [2026-07-08 17:53:20 UTC]

The recording confirmed the full model and I've amended the analysis. Here are your three asks, answered.

## The corrected model — a repeating two-primitive mechanic

Not a one-off hero treatment. Two primitives alternate:
- **REVEAL** — a higher z-layer section rises and *covers* what's beneath (explicit overlay).
- **STICKY** — a section's top row pins at `top:0` until the next reveal covers it off.

**Beat 1 — Reveal #1:** white grid rises over the fixed hero (boundary = 720px).
**Beat 2 — Sticky #1:** grid nav row pins (98.6px band).
**Beat 3 — Reveal #2:** dark Issue section rises and evicts the pinned nav.
**Beat 4 — Sticky #2:** table's search-toolbar + column-header row pin for the rest of the page.

## Your three asks

**1. Dark-section eviction uses the SAME overlay/z-index technique — confirmed.** I've recorded it explicitly as a *cover-up*, not a passive container-end release. The dark section gets a higher stacking context than the pinned nav so its rising edge overlaps and evicts the nav. Critically, this means Reveal #2 has to cover a *sticky* element, so the dark section's z-index must beat the nav's — noted as a build constraint.

**2. Reveal-#2 boundary measured (same method as the others):** the dark Issue section's top edge is at frame Y **2191.6**. Eviction *begins* when that edge meets the pinned nav's bottom — scroll ≈ **2093.0px** (2191.6 − 98.6 nav band) — and the nav is fully gone at **2191.6px** scrolled. I'll confirm the exact px at build.

Alongside it I locked **Beat 4's** numbers too: the table sticky band is the search toolbar (70px) + table padding (40px) + column-header row (25px) ≈ **135px**, with the first data row at frame Y **2837.6**.

**One correction to flag from my earlier analysis:** I'd said the table was "~55 rows, 6 columns" — the node tree confirms it's exactly **55 rows × 7 columns**, and row 1 is the column-header (the part that goes sticky).

**3. Shared vs. bespoke — my determination:**
- **Build the sticky primitives *into* the reusable blocks.** The site nav's `sticky` behavior belongs to the header block (every Page-Template screen has it); the table's sticky header belongs to the table block (the Home table *is* the Search page's table — same component). That way Calendar/Projects/Search/About inherit them for free.
- **Keep the reveal choreography bespoke to Home.** The fixed-hero → rising-grid → rising-dark z-layer eviction sequence (Beats 1–3) is unique to Home — no other frame has that stack. It stays a Home-scoped CSS+JS controller.
- **Caveat I want to preserve:** I'm inferring the other screens *don't* replicate Home's reveals, but I haven't verified their scroll behavior in Figma yet. I've noted to confirm each against the prototype when those pages are built, rather than assuming.

All of this is now in the import doc as the authoritative Home spec.

**Next step:** The Home model is fully specified and measured. The recording shows it covered early, but not whether it's still pinned underneath at the bottom. Which is it?

## Summary

Since the white sheet covers the hero completely, stay-vs-disappear is visually identical — so I'll use **`position: sticky` for the hero, not `fixed`**. That's the cleaner and more performant choice:
- Hero stays in normal flow (no phantom spacer div to fake its height)
- Pins at `top: 0`, gets covered by the higher-z white grid section
- The release is **pure CSS** — the browser handles it via the containing block, no scroll listeners for that beat

This sidesteps the question entirely rather than betting on either behavior. I've recorded both the technique and your rationale in the Home spec and as a project decision, so the build follows it directly.

**Summary of what changed:**

1. **Nav rides with the grid.** The nav no longer fades in — it now physically sits at the hero/grid seam, rides up 1:1 as the grid sheet scrolls over the hero, pins at the top, then slides off with the dark section. ### User [2026-07-08 21:05:48 UTC]

Ensure you truly implemented the following scrolling behavior:

Home page — scroll behavior specification

This describes the sticky/pinning behavior for the Home page, reverse-engineered frame-by-frame from a screen recording of the Figma prototype ([INT] Prada Index Website Design, file key 6WyrWzRXskdPTcPtQ296CQ, Home frame node 1:13091). No motion is authored explicitly in Figma's own interaction panel for this — it has to be inferred from the recorded scroll and implemented as bespoke CSS/JS.

The four content zones (in DOM/scroll order)

1. Hero (Figma node 1:13092) — full-bleed image + a thin masthead/ticker row above it (event listings + the PRADA wordmark).
2. Index grid section (1:13099) — a header row (the "Prada Index" wordmark + primary nav: Calendar, Projects, a search icon, About, with a thin rule beneath) followed by a 2-column-wide card grid of index entries (interviews, essays, photo essays).
3. Issue banner (1:13138) — a full-bleed dark/black section: "Issue 1 / Fall 2026" heading + description + an About CTA on the left, and a mockup preview panel of the print/PDF cover on the right.
4. Data table (1:13150) — a table of entries with a SEARCH … CLEAR toolbar row above the column headers (Project / Title / Type / Collaborator / Venue / Date / Producer), then data rows.

Behavior, precisely

Zone 1 → Zone 2 handoff (hero gets covered):
The hero is position: fixed (or an equivalent pinned-backdrop technique — e.g. a spacer element reserving its height while the visual sits fixed to the viewport) and sits at the lowest stacking layer. Zone 2 (the index grid section, with a solid/opaque background) scrolls up on a higher z-index, so as the user scrolls, zone 2 visually rises and progressively covers the hero from the bottom edge upward. The hero does not shrink, move, or fade — it stays exactly where it is; only the amount of it left uncovered changes. This is a full cover-up, not a scroll-past: once zone 2's leading edge reaches the top of the viewport, the hero is 100% hidden and effectively out of the picture.

Zone 2's own header row goes sticky:
Once the hero is fully covered, the top row of zone 2 (the "Prada Index" wordmark + nav) becomes position: sticky; top: 0 relative to zone 2's own container. It pins at the top of the viewport while the index card grid scrolls underneath it. Measured height of this header row elsewhere in this project's foundation work is ≈98.6px (see PROJECT-DESIGN.md --nav-height token) — treat that as the likely target here too, subject to verification against this specific frame's own header dimensions, which may differ from the global site nav.

Zone 2 → Zone 3 handoff (the sticky nav gets covered, NOT just scrolled past):
This is the detail most likely to be missed: when zone 3 (the dark Issue banner) arrives, it does not simply scroll up underneath the still-pinned nav and let it stay there indefinitely, nor does the nav's sticky behavior just "end" when zone 2's container runs out in the way a plain position: sticky element naturally releases. Instead, zone 3 rises on a higher stacking layer than the pinned nav — the exact same reveal technique used for the hero in the first handoff — and progressively covers/evicts the nav as zone 3's own top edge reaches the viewport top. Sequence observed: (a) zone 3 first appears and scrolls up normally, visible underneath the still-pinned nav, with no visual interaction between them; (b) once zone 3's rising edge reaches the nav's position, the nav starts getting cropped from the top down, as if a new opaque layer is sliding up in front of it; (c) once fully covered, the nav is completely gone from view. Zone 3 itself has no sticky content of its own — after finishing this eviction, it just continues scrolling normally like ordinary in-flow content until it scrolls off the top edge.

Zone 3 → Zone 4 transition (plain, no overlay reveal needed):
Zone 4 (the data table) does not need an overlay/reveal trick to take over the top of the viewport, because by the time it arrives, nothing is pinned above it to evict (zone 3 already scrolled away as ordinary content, with no sticky header surviving it). Zone 4's own header block — the SEARCH … CLEAR toolbar plus the column-header row — simply becomes position: sticky; top: 0 relative to zone 4's own container. It does not lock into place the instant it's the topmost content; it scrolls normally at first, and only visually "sticks" once continued scrolling would otherwise push it above the viewport top (ordinary CSS sticky behavior, no z-index tricks required). Once locked, it remains pinned at the top for the rest of the table, with data rows scrolling continuously underneath it.

Net structural model

- Two independent overlay-reveal transitions, both using the same mechanic (higher-stacking-layer section rises and covers whatever sat above it): hero→zone-2, and zone-2's-nav→zone-3.
- Two independent native sticky headers, each scoped to its own section container, activating only once their section is fully in view: zone-2's nav row, and zone-4's search/column-header row.
- At no point are two of these pinned/sticky elements visible simultaneously — every handoff is a clean replacement, not a stack.
- Zone 1 (hero) and zone 3 (Issue banner) never carry sticky content themselves; they are purely "backdrop" layers that get covered up or scroll away.

Implementation notes for whoever builds this

- Known CSS trap: overflow: hidden on any ancestor of a position: sticky element disables the sticky behavior entirely. If a wrapping container needs overflow control for another reason, use overflow-x: clip instead so the vertical sticky positioning still works (see this project's css-pitfalls-eds skill).
- The reveal-overlay technique (hero and Issue-banner eviction) is a distinct mechanism from position: sticky — it likely needs each covering section to have a solid background and a higher z-index/stacking context than the layer beneath it, with the covered layer fixed/pinned underneath.
- This is very likely bespoke Home-page CSS/JS, not a reusable block or variant — the hero-cover and Issue-banner-eviction beats are specific to Home's composition. However, the zone-4 pattern (a data table with its own sticky SEARCH/column-header row) may recur on other pages: this project's page-template inventory notes that Calendar, Projects, Search, and About all share one common "Page Template," and Calendar in particular is itself primarily a data table. If so, the zone-4 sticky-table-header behavior should be built once as part of that shared Page Template rather than duplicated as Home-specific code — confirm this before implementing.
- Verify the exact pixel boundaries for both reveal transitions (where zone 2 fully covers the hero; where zone 3 fully covers the nav) by measurement against the live Figma frame, the same way other foundation measurements in this project were locked from the source rather than estimated.
- Respect prefers-reduced-motion and confirm the behavior degrades sensibly on touch devices (no hover-dependent triggers; scroll-driven only).

### Assistant [2026-07-08 22:34:22 UTC]

## Summary

I built a **spacing scale from the Figma** and applied it systematically across the whole design.

**The scale** (in `styles.css :root`, measured from the frames — the site really does reuse the same handful of gaps):

| Token | Value | Where it recurs |
|---|---|---|
| `--space-2xs` | 8 | ribbon cell pad, label line gap |
| `--space-xs` | 10 | grid label inset, search-bar pad |
| `--space-s` | 12 | **all grid & column gutters** |
| `--space-m` | 20 | stacked headings, CTA pad, nav gaps, rule break |
| `--space-l` | 36 | banner desc→CTA |
| `--space-xl` | 48 | banner heading→body |
| `--space-2xl` | 80 | dark-banner bottom |

plus the existing `--page-padding` (40, base module) and `--block-padding` (24). Every block — columns, grid, hero, search-table, header — now draws from these instead of one-off px. Only true component geometry (icon sizes, 1px hairlines, table cell `6px 8px`, the 18px display-line gap) stays raw.

## Learnings consolidated

I reviewed the session against the capture bar and found **two genuinely new, non-obvious gotchas** worth adding — plus several that were *already* captured in earlier turns (the native-DOM nav relocation and the reveal-covers-sticky-nav / z-index specificity trap both went into `nav-header-eds` when they happened, so no duplication needed).

**1. `vertical-spacing-system` — stray colored band between full-bleed sections.** This bit *twice* in one build (black band above the relocated nav, then below the hero), which is the classic "same problem twice = skill gap" signal. Added a match-and-refuse pitfall: when a section's surface color lives on its inner `> div` but the `.section` shows a different ground, the global `--section-padding` renders as a stray stripe — zero the offending side (`padding-block: 0` for full-bleed), and verify with a seam probe.

**2. `css-pitfalls-eds` — aligning one grid's columns to another grid's.** The header-to-content-grid alignment took three failed measurement passes before it worked. Captured the three co-required fixes (`minmax(0,1fr)` not `1fr`; `min-width:0` on items; clear inherited mobile `grid-template-areas`/`grid-area`, including from higher-specificity state selectors) with the "unequal tracks like `0px 1072px 165px`" symptom to recognize it.

I updated both skills' `description` triggers and their `README.md` index rows so they auto-load next time.

**What I deliberately did *not* turn into a skill:** the spacing-token scale and symmetric-inset values are project-specific and already live in `PROJECT-DESIGN.md` (that's the right home per the rules — skills must not hardcode project values). The `.plain.html` nav/footer-fragment metadata mechanic is derivable from the code, so no capture.

One judgment call for you: the **symmetric-inset *principle*** ("insets that read as the same gap should be the same token, achieved via section padding rather than faked edge-to-edge") is arguably generic enough to belong in a design skill rather than only in PROJECT-DESIGN.md. Want me to lift that principle into a generic skill (e.g. alongside `vertical-spacing-system`), or leave it as a project note for now?

## Summary

**1. Nav + footer now auto-include — no per-page metadata.**
`header.js` and `footer.js` resolve their own fragment paths: `<contentPrefix>/nav` and `<contentPrefix>/footer`, where the prefix is `/content` on the local dev server and empty in production. I removed the nav-metadata blocks from all content pages and re-enabled the footer load. Verified on About (a plain page): both the nav bar and the footer appear with zero metadata in the page.

**2. Home is now a proper page template.**
- Home carries a `template: homepage` metadata block → `body.homepage`.
- The nav-relocation JS is gated on `body.homepage` (not on sniffing for `.hero-cover`), so the "nav lives in the 2nd section" logic runs **only** on the homepage.
- Converted all 10 Home-scoped CSS rules from `main:has(.hero-cover)` → `body.homepage main`.
- Added a `scripts.js` fallback (adds `body.homepage` if a `.hero-cover` is present) as a backup, per the `page-template-metadata` skill's "wire primary + fallback" rule.

Verified in preview: `body.homepage` is set, the header is relocated into the grid sheet (choreography intact), the footer renders, and other pages get the header at the top normally. CSS + JS lint both clean, and I updated PROJECT-BLOCKS.md to document the new auto-include and template mechanisms.

One thing worth flagging for later: the `scripts.js` homepage fallback currently keys off `.hero-cover`. That's fine as a safety net, but the **primary** mechanism is the authored metadata — when we eventually wire an importer (there's no import script on this Figma-only project yet), it should emit that `template: homepage` metadata so a re-import can't silently drop it. Want me to proceed to Phase 2 styling of the new pages' blocks (`event-list`, `project-header`, `masthead`), or is there anything else on the template/auto-include work first?

### User [2026-07-09 06:22:48 UTC]

You had added a padding bottom to the section containing the projects on the home page: body.homepage main > .section:first-child + .section > div { padding-bottom: var(--space-2xl); } One issue is that this padding bottom doesn't have the same size than the padding right and left of these white sections. It should instead be --page-padding. Also, it's not only this section that needs a padding bottom, but all sections. Therefore rather update the `main > .section > div` and set on it a padding all around of the same size: padding: var(--page-padding); The only exception to retain is that we generally don't want a padding top on the section of the homepage where the nav is included: body.homepage main > .section:first-child + .section > div { padding-top: 0; }
This might also allow to update main > .section.search-table-container > div

### Assistant [2026-07-09 07:59:10 UTC]

Now I understand both root causes clearly.

**Root cause 1 — "Search" label shows in production:** The deployed nav fragment (`/nav.plain.html`) is **missing the `class="nav-search"`** on the `<li>` — it's just `<li><a href="/search">…`. Our repo has `<li class="nav-search">`. The CSS that hides the text (`header nav .nav-search { font-size: 0 }`) never matches, so "Search" shows. This is a **stale published nav fragment** — the content wasn't re-published after we added the class.

**Root cause 2 — producer logos don't display:** In DA/production, authored images are transformed into **hashed asset references** (`media_<hash>.png`) served from the site root. But the producer logos are injected by JS (`producer-logos.js`) pointing at a **literal path** `/media/producers/fondazione-prada.png` — which returns 404 because that literal file was never published as an addressable asset (only content-referenced images get hashed + served). The logos live under `content/media/producers/` in the repo but aren't part of the code deployment (only `/icons`, `/blocks`, `/scripts`, `/styles` etc. are), nor are they DA assets.

Let me confirm what IS served from the code repo root in production.

### Assistant [2026-07-09 07:59:23 UTC]

Confirmed. Code-repo paths (`/icons`, `/blocks`, `/scripts`) serve fine from production, but `/media/producers/*` (which lives under `content/`, not the code repo) does not. The logos need to be hosted where production can serve them.

The cleanest fix that works in both environments: **move the producer logos into the code repo's `/icons` folder** (which is already deployed and served at the site root in production, and at `/icons` locally too — no `/content` prefix juggling), and point the helper there. Both fixes are done. Here's the diagnosis and what I changed:

## Summary

**Issue 1 — "Search" label showing in production (fix requires a republish, not a code change):**
The deployed nav fragment (`/nav.plain.html`) is missing `class="nav-search"` on the search `<li>` — it's just `<li><a href="/search">…`. Our repo *does* have `<li class="nav-search">` (the class the CSS uses to collapse the text via `font-size: 0`). So the published nav is **stale** — it predates when we added that class. **No code fix needed** — you just need to re-publish the `/nav` fragment from the Console; the current repo/DA source is already correct.

**Issue 2 — producer logos 404 in production (fixed in code):**
The logos were injected by JS pointing at `/media/producers/*.png`. That path 404s in production because `content/media/` files are only served via DA's *hashed* asset references (`media_<hash>.png`) when referenced from authored content — a literal JS path to them isn't addressable. Fix: I moved the three logos into the code repo's **`/icons`** folder (`producer-fondazione-prada.png`, `producer-prada.png`, `producer-luna-rossa.png`) and pointed `scripts/producer-logos.js` at `/icons/…`. `/icons` is served at the site root in **both** local and production (verified: `/icons/search.svg` = 200 live), so this also let me drop the `contentPrefix` juggling. Verified locally: all 54 logos load. This fix will take effect on production once the code branch is deployed.

**Also worth flagging (bigger cause of the live breakage):** the console on the live site shows it failing to load the **old block names** (`grid`, `search-table`) and the header/footer erroring — meaning the deployed site is significantly out of sync with the current code + content (renames, new blocks, template changes). Both flagged issues are symptoms of that drift. The real remedy is to **deploy the current code branch and re-publish the content** (nav fragment included) via the Console.

I can't deploy or publish myself — those are Console/Git actions you own. Want me to compile a short checklist of exactly what to re-publish (which fragments/pages) and confirm the code branch is deploy-ready?

### Assistant [2026-07-09 08:02:25 UTC]

This is the EDS anti-pattern: a `project-table` block (which authors create *as* a table) containing an HTML `<table>` — a nested table, terrible to author. The fix is a single table: the block's own rows hold the data cells directly, no inner `<table>`. Let me read the block's JS and CSS.

### Assistant [2026-07-09 08:03:07 UTC]

This is the classic EDS anti-pattern: the `project-table` block is *itself* authored as a table (block rows = table rows), and we nested an HTML `<table>` inside it — a table in a table. The fix: author the block's own rows as the data rows directly (single block-table), and have the JS build the semantic `<table>` for rendering/a11y. Let me see the full extent of the content to convert it.

### Assistant [2026-07-09 08:03:28 UTC]

## Summary

**The nested-table anti-pattern is fixed.** The `project-table` was authored as an HTML `<table>` wrapped inside the block's cells — a table-in-a-table, which is exactly the miserable-to-author structure you flagged. I converted it to a **single block-table**:

- **Content** (`content/index.plain.html` + the library page): the block's own rows are now the data rows — each `<div>` row holds 7 cell `<div>`s (one per column), with the first row carrying the column labels. No inner `<table>` anywhere.
- **Block JS** (`project-table.js`): builds the semantic `<table>`/`<th>`/`<td>` from those block rows (first row → header, rest → data) for rendering + accessibility, then proceeds with the injected search bar, live filter, and producer-logo swap as before.
- No CSS change needed — the block still produces a `<table>`.

Verified: exactly **1 table, 0 nested tables**, 7 header cells, 54 data rows, all 54 producer logos load, search filters 54→6 and Clear restores. Lint clean (JS + CSS).

**Documented the rule clearly everywhere content structuring is taught**, as you asked:
- **AGENTS.md Named Rules** — added **The No-Nested-Tables Rule** (citable, load-bearing).
- **`eds-content-modeling`** (the content-modeling hub) — full rule in the content-model section + a match-and-refuse pitfall.
- **`eds-dom-structure`** (the block-DOM skill) — added it right where it explains that a block's rows *are* a serialized table + a pitfall.
- **`skills/README.md`** index row updated to surface the rule.
- **PROJECT-BLOCKS.md** and **PROJECT-IMPORT.md** updated to describe the block as a single block-table.

The rule, stated plainly: **an EDS block already *is* a table (rows × cells), so tabular data uses the block's own rows as the table rows — never an HTML `<table>` (or another block) nested inside a block cell.** The only exception is a deliberate container block.

Does this fully resolve the nesting concern? I can sweep the other blocks/pages to confirm none carry the same pattern if you'd like.
